<div id="review_629789509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/629789509" />
    <a title="Gendou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1339246-gendou"><img alt="Gendou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229408059p2/1339246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/629789509?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gendou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gendou" href="/user/show/1339246-gendou">Gendou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1339246-gendou?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1339246-gendou?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer629789509&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer629789509" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16186287575146964692">The forward to this book promises "more than science fiction" but, alas, the book delivers something rather less. Part of the problem is, the book is old, written in 1937, before some scientific discoveries were widely accepted/known, and before others were even made. Also, the author isn't very science literate. Just enough to be dangerous.<br/><br/>Basically, the narrator goes on a magical journey through time and space, sees lots of aliens, and meets god "the star maker". The whole story is narrated, a</span>
  <span id="freeText16186287575146964692" style="display:none">The forward to this book promises "more than science fiction" but, alas, the book delivers something rather less. Part of the problem is, the book is old, written in 1937, before some scientific discoveries were widely accepted/known, and before others were even made. Also, the author isn't very science literate. Just enough to be dangerous.<br /><br />Basically, the narrator goes on a magical journey through time and space, sees lots of aliens, and meets god "the star maker". The whole story is narrated, and accomplished entirely by "telling" instead of "showing", though this doesn't bother me too much.<br /><br />A lot of the alien worlds are clever, if a bit half-baked. Take, for example, the tree people. They have an interesting life style, being rooted into place during the day, and free to walk around at night. But his evolutionary exobiology is terribly lacking. Any student of Darwin should find immediate fault with mobile autotrophs.<br /><br />The author uses a clever technique, to mix two things you don't see mixed on Earth (trees and people), then write about the hybrid product. But Stapledon didn't have the science necessary to do this justice.<br /><br />The author's writing in terms of vocabulary and prose are quite good. I suspect this is because he's an Englishman. But he uses lots of funny words like "human" and "man" to refer to aliens, which gets confusing. He's doing a British thing where "human" is synonymous with "humane", and where "man" implies a thinking mind.<br /><br />He uses other words that are shockingly different, in modern reading, than the author seems to have intended. For example, he uses "eugenics" to mean "genetic engineering", but since DNA was not discovered until 1950, I can't fault Stapledon's choice of words. Also, there are bizarre themes of Utopia and galactic Libertarianism that strike me as pitifully naive.<br /><br />The narrator just asks us to accept unjustified ideas like a whole star that is alive with its own "mind". Writing before the era of computers, he almost gets a pass, but... it's really dumb to say that a star is conscious. He doesn't seem to be up on his science. In the book, stars even get some parasite of their "biological tissues", which doesn't make sense, because no molecules can form in the plasma state of matter... anyway.<br /><br />For a book that is touted as fabulously open-minded and far-out, I found it terribly myopic (parochial?). The first aliens the narrator encounters are upright walkers who with drably human problems. The narrator makes excuses a lot. Here, he says that the psychic power that drew him to these human-like creatures similar to its possessor. Then, as he meets and is joined by similar creatures, each contributes some difference, so that the coverage of different forms grows ever outwards. Really, the author just needed a convenient way to arrange the book. This trope is lazy.<br /><br />About those psychic powers. Technology to link minds over large distances using radio would be a cool explanation. But, the powers also draw his consciousness back and forth through time. That sort of causality-violation can't be made paradox free, and so cannot exist as part of any serious science fiction book.<br /><br />The narrator makes many other poor excuses. When he runs out of ideas on a planet, he just says there isn't time to tell more about it. When something is impossible, the narrator begs forgiveness for being just one mind. When he was part of the traveling psychic space-mind, he could understand everything perfectly well. Yeah right.<br /><br />Towards the end of the book, we get a tale of god mixing up lots of universes, experimenting with different laws. This is where the story takes a turn for the inane. Star Maker has two separate "spirits" (god and the devil), which he is finally able to express satisfactorily in our universe.<br /><br />The digression into uninteresting theism is abrupt and has nothing to do with the rest of the book, except to explain away real problems with a non-answer. Why is there struggle and hardship? Oh, because God. Why are there so many galaxies full of rare and precious life? Right, because God. You might as well replace God with Boobs. Why Boobs? Because Boobs, that's why.<br /><br />Lest my scathingly positive review leave you confused, I enjoyed reading it (thus the 4-star rating) but find enormous fault in its contents. No contradiction there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16186287575146964692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_523357045" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/523357045" />
    <a title="Derek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1769635-derek"><img alt="Derek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232848809p2/1769635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/523357045?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek" href="/user/show/1769635-derek">Derek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11371994-star-maker">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer523357045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7503649625917998709"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2749148.Last_and_First_Men' title='Last and First Men by Olaf Stapledon' rel='nofollow noopener'> Last and First Men</a> hurt, but I'm back for more. And Stapledon continues to run with his vast future history, now encompassing the universe. It repeats the original structure, with a series of specific, detailed histories that eventually generalize and summarize, pulling back to show the entire grand scope. And in so doing, dares to slot the events of <i>Last and First Men</i>--the entirety of broadly-defined humanity's existence--as less than a footnote, never having joined galactic society and being </span>
  <span id="freeText7503649625917998709" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2749148.Last_and_First_Men" title="Last and First Men by Olaf Stapledon" rel="nofollow noopener"> Last and First Men</a> hurt, but I'm back for more. And Stapledon continues to run with his vast future history, now encompassing the universe. It repeats the original structure, with a series of specific, detailed histories that eventually generalize and summarize, pulling back to show the entire grand scope. And in so doing, dares to slot the events of <i>Last and First Men</i>--the entirety of broadly-defined humanity's existence--as less than a footnote, never having joined galactic society and being obliterated in Sol system by a stellar accident.<br /><br />But it is more than 'cosmic': this is a spiritual book. Its narrator continues to return to the topic of the Star Maker, the Supreme Being, and as stellar societies merge into galactic civilization and galactic mind, and half a million galaxies pull into synchronization and telepathic accord, the vast, inconceivable mental entity's only purpose is to commune with the Divinity and somehow reach meaning or answers or justification for all the failed species and suffering. And this here approaches something Lovecraftian, as this incomprehensibly vast mortal mind finally touches the source of all and realize how flawed and pitiful and wasteful the entire universe is: a rough draft for the next universal iteration towards some imperceptible goal. The Divine only perceives Its creation frostily, in analysis and infinitely remote dispassion, as It weaves aspects of morality itself into the work, as much as any physical property.<br /><br />In this, in its conclusion, is a narrator and perhaps an author coming to grips with the onset of the second World War, and the vast suffering and destruction that will be both necessary and regrettable. <br /><br />This is stark, detailed, frequently dry, and certainly exhausting literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7503649625917998709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_22921137" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22921137" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1185611-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22921137?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/1185611-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22921137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16311435826113140960">If a weird, science-named cult religion had spun off from this book, rather than by a pulp writer named Hubbard twenty years later, I would have no hair, no material possessions, and you would find me at your door with fliers to join. My only complaint is the heavy reliance on telepathy as the medium for movement and character interaction. Also, there isn't much plot, in any traditional sense, which is probably why no one ever reads this book. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1341538191" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1341538191" />
    <a title="Jerry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21430140-jerry"><img alt="Jerry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566521416p2/21430140.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1341538191?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jerry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jerry" href="/user/show/21430140-jerry">Jerry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780874774351" href="/book/show/2480464.Star_Maker">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21430140-jerry?shelf=writers-writers">writers-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1341538191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17049721070713350364"><em>Star Maker</em> must have been at least a little old-fashioned even when it was published in 1937. Stapledon uses the framing technique of Eddison and others, very much like Hodgson in <em>The House on the Borderland</em> to tell the story of a man who travels the cosmos by intellect alone. It is also more Wells than Wells in its didacticism. There isn’t much of a story here; it’s a journey of observation, explaining how the universe works, from the microscopic level to the divine.<br/><br/>Despite it being basically a</span>
  <span id="freeText17049721070713350364" style="display:none"><em>Star Maker</em> must have been at least a little old-fashioned even when it was published in 1937. Stapledon uses the framing technique of Eddison and others, very much like Hodgson in <em>The House on the Borderland</em> to tell the story of a man who travels the cosmos by intellect alone. It is also more Wells than Wells in its didacticism. There isn’t much of a story here; it’s a journey of observation, explaining how the universe works, from the microscopic level to the divine.<br /><br />Despite it being basically a fantasy, he attempts to keep the observations rooted in hard science. Since it was published in 1937, some of that science is outdated, such as how planets form.<br /><br />There are some elements of satire. For example, the first race he encounters uses taste much more extensively than we do, and different peoples are divided by taste rather than colors. Each group, of course, assumes themselves to be in the best taste. The industrial class has evolved to have near-uniformly low taste, according to those of good taste.<br /><br />This is a very imaginative but hopelessly socialist utopian novel. Some analogy like that to Marxist industrial class relations is repeated throughout every level of the cosmos. Every world undergoes Labor troubles regardless of how wildly different the world is.<br /><br />Judging from some of the entries in the glossary, this may have been an attempt to create a religion or at least spirituality in favor of communism. See the glossary entries for <em>Religion</em>, <em>Spiritual</em>, and <em>Worship</em>, for example. But the closer he gets to utopia, the more he displays the things that expose socialism for what it is, at root, in ways that give us a glimpse of not just an outdated worldview, but one that led to massive death and destruction.<br /><br />The worlds that survive to a greater consciousness all exhibit “a remarkable identity.”<br /><br /><blockquote>For instance, in the loosest possible sense, all were communistic; for in all of them the means of production were communally owned, and no individual could control the labour of others for private profit. Again, in a sense all these world-orders were democratic, since the final sanction of policy was world-opinion. But in many cases there was no democratic machinery, no legal channel for the expression of world-opinion. Instead, a highly specialized bureaucracy, or even a world-dictator, might carry out the business of organizing the world’s activity with legally absolute power, but under constant supervision by popular will expressed through the radio.</blockquote><br /><br />His utopia is a dictatorship controlled by the media. But that’s not the worst of it. Barring an astronomical disaster, this enlightened world,<br /><br /><blockquote>In this happy phase, then, which might last for a few centuries or for many thousands of years, the whole energy of the world would be devoted to perfecting the world-community and raising the caliber of the race by cultural and by eugenical means.</blockquote><br /><br />In 1937. Paging <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88634.Jonah_Goldberg" title="Jonah Goldberg" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonah Goldberg</a>.<br /><br />Sometimes this eugenics leads to developing individuals who, when they detect themselves disagreeing too much with a newer generation, will “thereupon crave and practice euthanasia, gladly yielding his place to a successors of more modern type.”<br /><br />Quoting only these snippets might give the impression of clumsy satire, but in context this is the author’s utopia.<br /><br />When the narrator is looking over the earth, he over-emphasizes “the legend of Lenin” and twice mentions some great work of Lenin called Magnetostroy.<br /><br /><blockquote>There Lenin lay, victorious. Far off, at the foot of the Urals, imagination detected the ruddy plumes and smoke-pall of Magnetostroy.<br /><br />Ancient Memphis itself now echoed with the rumour of Magnetostroy.</blockquote><br /><br />I had never heard of Magnetostroy, and passed over the first entry thinking it might be some Russian city renamed by the communists and since returned to its pre-communist name. On reading the word a second time, I decided it was important enough verify my guess, so I googled it. What comes up is Ozymandian in its obscurity. Three of the results are from <em>Star Maker</em>. Three more are a reddit and Wikipedia commenter who has taken it for their anonymous nickname. Two are things called magnetos in Troy, Kansas and Troy, Illinois.<br /><br />The one that is likely what Stapledon refers to I found under a piece of artwork from 1931, <em>Victory of the 5 Year Plan - A blow to Capitalism</em>. It was apparently some construction, perhaps a factory, that is all but forgotten today.<br /><br />The saving grace of the story is the imaginative way he populates the universe with a wide variety of very strange creatures and symbiotic relationships. Because of the scope of the novel, however, few get more than a short passage, or get to do anything in the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17049721070713350364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_376516613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/376516613" />
    <a title="Ed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93079-ed"><img alt="Ed" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/376516613?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed" href="/user/show/93079-ed">Ed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer376516613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1263064755910221767">I got this from a list of books on io9 that were "books everyone talks about and no one reads." It was apparently very influential, and I can see why.<br/><br/>First the negative: Like most old fiction (1930s here), this book has a problem with pacing. For the most part it reads like a history textbook, with occasional personal interjections and foreshadowing along the lines of, "Pay attention, these guys will be important later." <br/><br/>That said, it's interesting enough to overcome that issue and keep you (me)</span>
  <span id="freeText1263064755910221767" style="display:none">I got this from a list of books on io9 that were "books everyone talks about and no one reads." It was apparently very influential, and I can see why.<br /><br />First the negative: Like most old fiction (1930s here), this book has a problem with pacing. For the most part it reads like a history textbook, with occasional personal interjections and foreshadowing along the lines of, "Pay attention, these guys will be important later." <br /><br />That said, it's interesting enough to overcome that issue and keep you (me) reading. The book starts out as an interstellar travelogue and becomes a meditation on the nature of reality. The travelogue part was fascinating, and the meditation part seemed pretty prescient, at least with respect to certain current pop science ideas. The author clearly makes an effort to get the astronomy right, but since this is 30s astronomy, some parts are laughably wrong.<br /><br />The book also has an interesting historical context, in that it was written during the depression in the lead up to World War II. This clearly influences the story, explicitly so in some cases.<br /><br />So yeah, good book, somewhat awkwardly written, and no wonder it's been influential. It's so full of ideas you could write thirty different books based on the cosmos described here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1263064755910221767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ollie by: </span>
      it was a Xmas present

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82012851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1341068501573472463">This book nearly blew my head off so I can only imagine what readers felt when they first encountered it in 1937. Enthralled? Ecstatic? Spellbound? If I were Charlton Heston in <i>Planet of the Apes</i> and I found a copy of this book underneath the fallen Statue of Liberty, I'd create a religion around it. It is, after all, a beautiful example of how science fiction can touch theology and make the reader believe momentarily that there is meaning to life.<br/><br/>On a silent, starry night, the narrator of the n</span>
  <span id="freeText1341068501573472463" style="display:none">This book nearly blew my head off so I can only imagine what readers felt when they first encountered it in 1937. Enthralled? Ecstatic? Spellbound? If I were Charlton Heston in <i>Planet of the Apes</i> and I found a copy of this book underneath the fallen Statue of Liberty, I'd create a religion around it. It is, after all, a beautiful example of how science fiction can touch theology and make the reader believe momentarily that there is meaning to life.<br /><br />On a silent, starry night, the narrator of the novel finds himself abandoning his corporal form on a hillside in exchange for a trip around the galaxy - and then the universe(s) - that will bring him close to the truths about creation. Stapledon does a great job of capturing the sense of wonder, adventure and discovery that the narrator goes through as his consciousness expands with each new civilization and scientific discovery he encounters. As the history of our universe becomes clear to the narrator, a path opens up to the "Star Maker" - the being behind the creation of everything.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1341068501573472463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9031502917595680870">As a work of the imagination, I have never read anything that surpasses this. As a 'science fiction' novel this is definitely in my top ten although it is important to remember that Stapledon was not aware that he was writing anything resembling a work in this genre and indeed had never heard of the genre at all.<br/><br/>This is such a profound book that it seems like a terrible shame that it has only attracted a relatively small reading audience: it deserves much more than the Happy Few who currently co</span>
  <span id="freeText9031502917595680870" style="display:none">As a work of the imagination, I have never read anything that surpasses this. As a 'science fiction' novel this is definitely in my top ten although it is important to remember that Stapledon was not aware that he was writing anything resembling a work in this genre and indeed had never heard of the genre at all.<br /><br />This is such a profound book that it seems like a terrible shame that it has only attracted a relatively small reading audience: it deserves much more than the Happy Few who currently compose its fan base.</span>

<div id="review_477426223" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/477426223" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9582846-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551699829p2/9582846.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/477426223?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/9582846-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer477426223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10598385854989616028">I've never written a review for a book before, but this one left such a strong impression on me that I think I should write one.<br/><br/>First of all, it is not an easy read. The whole book is essentially prose-poetry. There is no dialogue - every page is filled with rich, detailed, poetic descriptions. Not necessarily a bad thing, but it meant that it took me a while longer to finish the book.<br/><br/>Each page is also likely to contain some deep philosophical or spiritual idea. After reading a page or two I nee</span>
  <span id="freeText10598385854989616028" style="display:none">I've never written a review for a book before, but this one left such a strong impression on me that I think I should write one.<br /><br />First of all, it is not an easy read. The whole book is essentially prose-poetry. There is no dialogue - every page is filled with rich, detailed, poetic descriptions. Not necessarily a bad thing, but it meant that it took me a while longer to finish the book.<br /><br />Each page is also likely to contain some deep philosophical or spiritual idea. After reading a page or two I need to stop just to be able to fully appreciate the idea that Stapledon is trying to convey.<br /><br />In some ways, this is the ultimate sci-fi book. It contains references to actual astronomical facts that were known during the time it was written. It also includes many, if not all, of the classic sci-fi themes: interstellar travel, telepathy, virtual reality, alien invasion, alien civilisation, time travel, dystopian and utopian societies, so forth and so on.<br /><br />But what makes this book so great for me is how many deep reflective moments there are in the book. Stapledon contemplates human nature, the rise and fall of civilisation, collective consciousness (between humans and alien species), our place in the Universe, competing political ideologies, the origins and nature of religions and gods, mysticism, and (possibly the most mind-boggling concept in the whole book) the idea that planets, stars, galaxies and the Universe itself could be conscious as a kind of super-mind. I love the way he convincingly details how planets, stars and galaxies can have their own unique intelligence and form their own kind of civilisation.<br /><br />I don't know if Stapledon was familiar with Eastern mysticism and philosophy, but many of the concepts he explores are similar to concepts found in Hinduism, Buddhism and Taoism. These concepts include ethical teachings central to Eastern philosophy, such as altruism, compassion and care for the community. Other Eastern concepts explored include the nature of the Self, self-transcendence and the desire to reach a higher plane of existence. <br /><br />What really surprised me was how psychedelic and trippy many of the ideas and descriptions were. When the protagonist, for example, finally confronts the 'Star Maker', it sounds like an intense psychedelic or religious experience. In fact, many of his descriptions are incredibly psychedelic. I just can't believe that these descriptions are not influenced by a high dose of a drug such as LSD, mushrooms or from a DMT experience. <br /><br />Lastly, I think the way in which the author describes the Universe - as something immensely vast and complex and mysterious - easily rivals the poetic descriptions of Carl Sagan, or anyone else actually. A bold statement, but I think it's true. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10598385854989616028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151576546" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151576546" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5062970-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299179754p2/5062970.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151576546?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/5062970-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151576546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18428191852337635376">This is true Science Fiction. That is, with the emphasis heavily weighed on Fiction. I find too many Sci-Fi authors get caught up with the Science wand miss out on the opportunity to narrative meaningful, allegoric and absorbing tales. Olaf Stapledon does not make this mistake. In fact, it was not until after its publication that he discovered the Science Fiction tendencies that the novel held (a genre he had hitherto never heard of). <br/><br/>Stapledon wrote this novel primarily as a way to explore phil</span>
  <span id="freeText18428191852337635376" style="display:none">This is true Science Fiction. That is, with the emphasis heavily weighed on Fiction. I find too many Sci-Fi authors get caught up with the Science wand miss out on the opportunity to narrative meaningful, allegoric and absorbing tales. Olaf Stapledon does not make this mistake. In fact, it was not until after its publication that he discovered the Science Fiction tendencies that the novel held (a genre he had hitherto never heard of). <br /><br />Stapledon wrote this novel primarily as a way to explore philosophical, theological ideas about existence, both individually, and as a community. He also, by proxy wanted to explore the problems and advantageous possibilities of communication between the individuals that make up a community. So, he turns to the vast expanse of the universe, where he is able to explore these themes in the context of science, using the unknown as means of creating various races, communities, empires and galactic wars that take place across the history of our universe in order to expand upon the themes that he wants to explore. This is Sci-Fi as it is meant to be, a means to explore aspects of the human experience in unconventional manners.<br /><br />Stapledon tries to do all of this in a mere 250 pages. It is an Epic task, yet not once does it feel rushed, for all its grandiose proportion and spiritual musings it never falls into the absurd. It remains consistent, thought provoking, a beautiful, majestic and concise portrayal of philosophical ideas that never amount to incoherent, pretentious preaching and, most importantly for the general reader, this novel is perhaps one of the most imaginative adventures ever written (certainly in my experience.) <br /><br />It is simply wondrous. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18428191852337635376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1631492-star-maker">Quotes from Star Maker</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Sitting there on the heather, on our planetary grain, I shrank from the abysses that opened up on every side, and in the future. The silent darkness, the featureless unknown, were more dread than all the terrors that imagination had mustered. Peering, the mind could see nothing sure, nothing in all human experience to be grasped as certain, except uncertainty itself; nothing but obscurity gendered by a thick haze of theories. Man's science was a mere mist of numbers; his philosophy but a fog of words. His very perception of this rocky grain and all its wonders was but a shifting and a lying apparition. Even oneself, that seeming-central fact, was a mere phantom, so deceptive, that the most honest of men must question his own honesty, so insubstantial that he must even doubt his very existence.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/546518-sitting-there-on-the-heather-on-our-planetary-grain-i">22 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;All this long human story, most passionate and tragic in the living, was but an unimportant, a seemingly barren and negligible effort, lasting only for a few moments in the life of the galaxy. When it was over, the host of the planetary systems still lived on, with here and there a casualty, and here and there among the stars a new planetary birth, and here and there a fresh disaster.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/539417-all-this-long-human-story-most-passionate-and-tragic-in">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
